### [46] [SHAP-Weighted Cross-Modal Expert Fusion for Emotion and Sentiment Recognition: Evidence and Limits](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.08573)
*Adis Alihodzic,Selma Skopljakovic Hubljar*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper analyzes XAI-guided adaptive fusion (XGAF) for multimodal emotion/sentiment recognition, focusing on how SHAP attribution reduction methods affect fusion performance when experts have unequal dimensionalities, finding sum-abs reduction outperforms mean/median methods and nearly matches early fusion performance while maintaining modularity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing fusion methods for multimodal emotion/sentiment recognition have trade-offs: early fusion is accurate but monolithic, while late fusion is modular but loses cross-modal interactions. The paper aims to investigate XAI-guided adaptive fusion as a balanced approach, specifically examining how SHAP attribution reduction affects performance when experts have different feature dimensionalities.

Method: The paper revisits XAI-guided adaptive fusion (XGAF), a tree-based mixture of unimodal and cross-modal experts where sample-level weights are derived from TreeSHAP attribution magnitudes. It focuses on three different SHAP attribution reduction methods (mean-abs, median-abs, and sum-abs) and evaluates them on MELD (7-class emotion recognition) and CMU-MOSEI (3-class sentiment recognition) datasets. The method includes ablation studies to identify sources of performance gains and diagnostics to understand weight distribution patterns.

Result: On MELD, sum-abs XGAF achieves 0.5983 weighted F1, nearly matching early fusion's 0.6018 and significantly outperforming late fusion's 0.4598 (p<0.0001). McNemar testing shows no significant difference between sum-abs XGAF and early fusion (p=1.000). On CMU-MOSEI, sum-abs XGAF reaches 0.6519 weighted F1, slightly exceeding early fusion (0.6485) and late fusion (0.5696). Mean-abs and median-abs reductions suppress high-dimensional cross-modal experts, while sum-abs preserves total attribution mass, concentrating weights on the trimodal expert and enabling modular fusion with near-early-fusion performance.

Conclusion: The main contribution is a transparent empirical analysis showing that sum-abs SHAP reduction, when combined with cross-modal expert design (particularly trimodal experts), enables modular multimodal fusion that nearly matches monolithic early fusion performance. This demonstrates that XAI-guided adaptive fusion can achieve the modularity benefits of late fusion while approaching the performance of early fusion, offering a practical solution to the accuracy-modularity trade-off in multimodal learning.

Abstract: Multimodal emotion and sentiment recognition is commonly addressed by early fusion, which concatenates modalities before classification, or late fusion, which combines independently trained unimodal predictors. Early fusion can be accurate but monolithic, while late fusion is modular but may lose cross-modal interactions. This paper revisits XAI-guided adaptive fusion (\xgaf), a tree-based mixture of unimodal and cross-modal experts whose sample-level weights are derived from TreeSHAP attribution magnitudes. We focus on the effect of SHAP attribution reduction when experts have unequal feature dimensionalities. In this setting, mean-abs and median-abs reductions can suppress high-dimensional cross-modal experts, whereas sum-abs reduction preserves total attribution mass. On MELD 7-class emotion recognition, sum-abs \xgaf{} nearly matches early fusion across three face-sequence aggregators; the Transformer variant reaches 0.5983 \wf{}, compared with 0.6018 for early fusion and 0.4598 for probability-average late fusion. McNemar testing shows no significant difference between sum-abs \xgaf{} and early fusion on MELD ($p=1.000$), while \xgaf{} remains significantly better than late fusion ($p<0.0001$). On CMU-MOSEI 3-class sentiment recognition, sum-abs \xgaf{} reaches 0.6519 \wf{}, slightly exceeding early fusion (0.6485) and late fusion (0.5696). Ablation studies show that the main gain comes from adding cross-modal experts, especially the trimodal expert, rather than from complex per-sample routing. Diagnostics further show that mean-abs and median-abs weights are nearly uniform, while sum-abs weights concentrate on the trimodal expert. Thus, the main contribution is a transparent empirical analysis of how SHAP reduction, expert dimensionality, and cross-modal expert design affect modular multimodal fusion.

We developed a patient simulator that separately models clinical content, emotional state, conversational strategy, and communication style. In a Turing-inspired evaluation of realism with 15 human graders, simulated conversations were nearly indistinguishable from real ones, with human graders achieving an accuracy of 55%. We used five distinct patient personae, across 1,164 clinician-graded cases, to evaluate the performance of four LLMs in urgency assessment. We found that communication style can significantly alter triage outcomes. ### [49] [Formal Mechanisms for Market Stability in Self-Interested Agent Societies: A Marketplace Simulation Study](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.08652)
*Eugene Ng Yi Sheng,Bingquan Shen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Mediation is a robust formal mechanism that enables self-interested LLM agents to maintain cooperative market stability under adversarial attacks, even with optimized troll agents.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unconstrained self-interested agents tend to defect in repeated social dilemmas, causing cooperative gains from trade to collapse. The paper investigates what formal mechanisms beyond unrestricted communication can maintain market stability and how resilient they are to adversarial attacks.

Method: Multi-agent marketplace simulation with 18 DeepSeek-V3 LLM agents with complementary production specialties trading within a constrained social network. Two experimental phases: (1) mechanism comparison across eight conditions under progressive troll injection over 200 rounds, and (2) adversarial red-teaming of Mediation using iteratively prompt-optimized LLM-driven trolls.

Result: Mediation was identified as the top-performing mechanism. The best adversarial attack (v6) reduced honest-agent utility by 13.3% but couldn't collapse the market. Mediation enables recovery even under sustained adversarial pressure.

Conclusion: Mediation is robust: it can be bent but not broken. It sustains positive honest-agent utility under optimized attacks, defining adversarial robustness as a mechanism's ability to maintain this positive utility under attack.

Abstract: Self-interested agents, left unconstrained, tend toward defection in repeated social dilemmas, causing cooperative gains from trade to collapse. This paper investigates what formal mechanisms, layered on top of unrestricted communication, are sufficient for a society of such agents to maintain market stability, and how resilient those mechanisms are to adversarial attack. We instantiate the research question as a multi-agent marketplace simulation where 18 LLM agents (DeepSeek-V3) with complementary production specialties must trade within a constrained social network to obtain utility. We conduct two experimental phases: (1) a mechanism comparison across eight conditions under progressive troll injection over 200 rounds, identifying Mediation as the top-performing mechanism; and (2) adversarial red-teaming of Mediation using iteratively prompt-optimised LLM-driven trolls, finding that the best attack (v6) reduces honest-agent utility by 13.3% but cannot collapse the market. Mediation enables recovery even under sustained adversarial pressure. We define adversarial robustness as a mechanism's ability to sustain positive honest-agent utility under optimised attack, and find that Mediation is robust: it can be bent but not broken.

</details>

*Shilin Ou,Yifan Xu,Luyao Zhang*

When physics penalties are removed, reward-maximizing agents exploit invalid generation and increase artificial liquidity. In decentralized energy markets, autonomous agents may improve market utility, but may also exploit invalid physical data, create artificial liquidity, and produce unstable governance decisions. It formulates market governance as a Gymnasium-compatible Markov Decision Process, where agents make hourly decisions. SolarChain-Eval evaluates each policy across multiple dimensions, including market utility, physical safety, slippage, action smoothness, spatial fairness, and auditability. To support agentic evaluation, SolarChain-Eval incorporates an LLM-based Planner/Auditor layer. The Planner defines episode-level action bounds and audit rules, while the Auditor reviews and revises high-risk actions. All interventions are recorded through structured logs, including trigger signals, proposed actions, revised actions, and audit rationales. Experiments with static, random, myopic, RL, and RL+LLM policies reveal a clear utility-safety trade-off. RL agents improve market utility but can still produce unsafe behavior. When the physics penalty is removed, reward-maximizing agents exploit invalid generation and increase artificial liquidity. The LLM Planner/Auditor improves auditability and mitigates selected risks, but it cannot fully compensate for a misspecified reward function. These results indicate that trustworthy agentic AI evaluation requires both physical constraints and transparent intervention traces. *Yifan Wu,Lizhu Zhang,Yuhang Zhou,Mingyi Wang,Bo Peng,Serena Li,Xiangjun Fan,Zhuokai Zhao*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: A separate memory agent runs alongside an unmodified action agent, updating a structured memory bank from recent trajectory and deciding whether to inject a memory-grounded reminder or remain silent. Training improves validation reward and achieves partial transfer to Terminal-Bench.

The proposed plug-and-play memory module effectively prevents behavioral state decay in long-horizon tasks, demonstrating significant performance improvements across benchmarks and establishing groundwork for open-weight memory policies.

As trajectories grow, task requirements, environment facts, prior attempts, diagnoses, and open subgoals can be buried in the context window or pushed beyond it, failing to influence decisions when needed. We call this failure mode "behavioral state decay". We study memory as an active intervention mechanism rather than passive retrieval. A separate memory agent runs alongside an unmodified action agent, updating a structured memory bank from the recent trajectory and deciding whether to inject a memory-grounded reminder or remain silent. The module is plug-and-play with frontier action agents and existing agent harnesses. Across Terminal-Bench 2.0 and $τ^2$-Bench, it improves pass@1 for both weaker and stronger action agents, with gains of +8.3 pp on Terminal-Bench and +6.8 pp on $τ^2$-Bench. Ablations show that selective intervention outperforms passive bank exposure, always-on injection, advisor-only guidance, and general retrieval. As an early step toward open-weight memory policies, we train Qwen3.5-27B on SETA using SFT and GRPO, improving validation reward and achieving partial transfer to Terminal-Bench.

### [52] [The Illusion of Equivalency: Statistical Characterization of Quantization Effects in LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.08734)
*Baha Rababah,Cuneyt Gurcan Akcora,Carson K. Leung*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Quantization changes LLM behavior even when accuracy metrics appear unchanged, revealing hidden behavioral divergence that requires new evaluation metrics beyond traditional accuracy/perplexity.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current evaluation of quantized large language models relies almost exclusively on accuracy and perplexity metrics, which fail to capture behavioral changes induced by quantization. There's a need to understand how quantization affects model behavior beyond surface-level performance metrics.

Method: Introduced correctness agreement metric (decision-level overlap in correct predictions between base and quantized models). Analyzed multiple models across quantization schemes (8-bit to 2-bit). Studied quantization as structural operator on attention weights using statistical and distributional measures to quantify layer-wise distortions.

Result: Behavioral divergence emerges under moderate quantization even when task performance appears preserved. Found non-linear breakpoints at low bit-widths. Query and key projections are consistently more sensitive to quantization than value and output projections. Traditional metrics create illusion of equivalence between base and quantized models.

Conclusion: Quantization causes significant behavioral changes that are missed by conventional accuracy/perplexity metrics. The paper exposes an illusion of equivalence and motivates behavioral evaluation beyond traditional performance metrics, revealing hidden vulnerabilities in quantized models.

Abstract: Post-training quantization is widely used to deploy large language models in resource-constrained settings, yet its evaluation relies almost exclusively on accuracy and perplexity. We show that these metrics fail to capture behavioral changes induced by quantization. We introduce correctness agreement, a decision-level metric that measures overlap in correct predictions between a base model and its quantized variants, independent of absolute accuracy. Across multiple models and quantization schemes from 8-bit to 2-bit, we find that behavioral divergence emerges under moderate quantization even when task performance appears preserved. To explain this effect, we analyze quantization as a structural operator on attention weights and quantify layer-wise distortions using statistical and distributional measures. Our results reveal non-linear breakpoints at low bit-widths and show that query and key projections are consistently more sensitive than value and output projections. These findings expose an illusion of equivalence between base and quantized models and motivate behavioral evaluation beyond conventional performance metrics.

### [53] [Workflow as Knowledge: Semantic Persistence for LLM-Mediated Workflows](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.08740)
*Emanuele Quinto,Carlo Andrea Rozzi,Francesco Zanitti*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes a Lisp-inspired conceptual model for LLM workflows that treats all workflow components as persistent knowledge objects, distinguishing between deterministic derivation and LLM-mediated inference.

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Proposes a language-independent conceptual model inspired by Lisp: uses symbolic forms, object identity, and live-image thinking as explanatory frameworks. Represents workflow definitions, instances, inference records, context snapshots, and dependencies as persistent knowledge objects in a shared knowledge substrate.

Result: Establishes a conceptual framework for semantic persistence where workflows are not just producers of knowledge but are themselves inspectable, resumable, and reviewable knowledge objects. Introduces the key semantic distinction between derive (deterministic computation) and infer (LLM-mediated judgment under context and capability policy).

Conclusion: The paper presents a preliminary conceptual account of semantic persistence that enables workflow introspection, resumption, and review, though formal transition semantics remain as future work.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) applications increasingly use explicit workflows for tool use, retrieval, branching, checkpointing, and human approval. Existing workflow systems already address many execution concerns. This paper proposes a Lisp-inspired but language-independent conceptual model: symbolic forms, object identity, and live-image thinking are used as explanatory lenses, not implementation commitments. In this model, workflow definitions, workflow instances, inference records, context snapshots, and dependency relations are represented as persistent knowledge objects in a shared knowledge substrate. Its central semantic distinction is between derive and infer: derive is deterministic computation over available state; infer is mediated LLM judgment under declared context and executor-controlled capability policy.
  The result is a preliminary conceptual account of semantic persistence: workflows do not merely produce knowledge and leave traces, but can themselves be represented as inspectable, resumable, and reviewable knowledge objects, while formal transition semantics remain future work.

*Yifan Zhou,Qihao Yang,Yan Li,Donggang Li,Xiru Hu,Hokin Deng,Ziyang Gong,Xuanyi Zhou,Huacan Wang,Xiangchao Yan,Wanghan Xu,Wenlong Zhang,Shaofeng Zhang,Yue Zhou,Yifan Yang,Zhihang Zhong,Xue Yang*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI benchmarks don't adequately test whether systems can understand and follow the inheritance structure of scientific ideas, where research builds upon, repairs, and recombines previous work similar to biological evolution.

Method: The authors developed the IdeaGene framework where papers are represented as minimal, typed, evidence-grounded Idea Genome objects. GenomeDiff records track six evolutionary dynamics: inheritance, mutation, loss, external import, and novel insertion. IG-Bench contains 1,961 golden lineage traces, 1,085 curated Idea Genome objects, and 920 pairwise GenomeDiff records across 10 scientific domains. It includes two evaluations: IG-Exam (42 task types) for lineage reasoning, and IG-Arena for generation using Population-Evolution Score.

Result: Experiments with 14 LLM-based scientists reveal a compositional bottleneck - the strongest system only achieves 27.3% exact accuracy on lineage reasoning. Structured lineage context reshuffles system rankings rather than uniformly helping all participants.

Conclusion: IG-Bench reveals significant gaps in current AI systems' ability to reason about scientific lineage and generate lineage-grounded ideas, highlighting the need for improved compositional reasoning capabilities in scientific AI systems.

Abstract: Scientific ideas rarely start from a blank page. They inherit mechanisms, repair known limitations, and recombine pieces of earlier work, much like biological genomes. Current benchmarks still say little about whether AI systems can follow this inheritance structure. We present IdeaGene-Bench (IG-Bench), a benchmark for scientific lineage reasoning and lineage-grounded idea generation. IG-Bench is organized around the IdeaGene framework: each paper or proposal is represented as a set of minimal, typed, evidence-grounded Idea Genome objects, and a GenomeDiff aligns these objects to record inheritance, mutation, loss, external import, and novel insertion under six operational evolutionary dynamics. The benchmark contains 1,961 golden lineage traces, 1,085 curated Idea Genome objects, and 920 pairwise GenomeDiff records across 10 scientific domains. It supports two evaluations. IG-Exam (42 task types, 1,029 instances) tests closed-form lineage reasoning across Idea Genome abstraction, inheritance tracing, evolutionary reasoning, and lineage verification. IG-Arena evaluates generation with a lineage-conditioned Population-Evolution Score(PES), asking whether a proposal can be inserted as a coherent descendant of a given lineage population: it should inherit the right Idea Genome objects, vary meaningfully from nearby work, and offer selection value for future research. Experiments on 14 LLM-based scientists expose a compositional bottleneck. The strongest system reaches only 27.3% exact accuracy on lineage reasoning, and structured lineage context reshuffles system rankings rather than helping every participant uniformly.

### [57] [Towards the Explainability of Temporal Graph Networks via Memory Backtracking and Topological Attribution](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07716)
*Yazheng Liu,Xi Zhang,Sihong Xie,Hui Xiong*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A novel explanation method for Temporal Graph Networks that attributes predictions by analyzing both topology interactions and historical event influences through memory backtracking, outperforming existing baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing explanation methods for Temporal Graph Networks overlook the crucial memory module that records and updates node histories, leaving the influence of past events unexplored, which hinders trustworthiness of TGN predictions.

Method: Two-stage attribution approach: 1) topology attribution tree captures influence of neighbors and their memory vectors, 2) memory backtracking tree quantifies how historical events shape node memory vectors. Applies LRP (Layer-wise Relevance Propagation) and designs optimization objectives to identify important events beyond simple top-k selection.

Result: Method provides faithful explanations and outperforms state-of-the-art baselines across nine temporal graph datasets, spanning node property prediction, link prediction, and graph classification tasks.

Conclusion: The proposed method successfully addresses the gap in explaining TGN predictions by accounting for memory module influences, enhancing model trustworthiness through comprehensive historical event attribution.

Abstract: Temporal graphs are ubiquitous in real-world applications and Temporal Graph Networks (TGNs) have achieved superior predictive accuracy. Understanding which historical events drive model predictions can enhance trustworthiness of TGNs. Existing explanation methods overlook the memory module, the core component that records and updates node histories, leaving the influence of past events unexplored. To address this, we attribute TGNs predictions through the topology attribution tree and memory backtracking tree. The topology attribution tree captures the influence of neighbors and their memory vectors, then the memory backtracking tree quantifies how historical events shape node memory vectors. We apply the LRP in TGNs, ensuring that the total contribution of events equals the logits of model. Finally, top-k selection may be unfaithful due to the nonlinear mapping from logits to probabilities, we design optimization objectives to identify the important events. Experiments on nine temporal graph datasets, spanning node property prediction, link prediction tasks and graph classification tasks, show that our method provides faithful explanations and outperforms state-of-the-art baselines. The code is available at https://github.com/yazhengliu/MemExplainer

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In chest X-ray classification, acceptable overall ranking performance can still leave rare-positive patients (particularly within subgroups) below detection thresholds, creating pre-deployment fairness problems. The study addresses this as an audit question: after converting a long-tailed multi-label CXR model from scores to decisions, who gets missed?

Method: The researchers use a diagnostic ladder to systematically analyze multiple factors: class-level long-tail losses, subgroup-aware weighting, group robustness methods, and threshold selection. They apply this approach across two datasets (VinDr-CXR and MIMIC-CXR/CXR-LT), comparing group-tail weighting followed by tail-aware thresholding against baseline approaches. They use paired bootstrap contrasts for statistical validation and compare with GroupDRO as a reference for group robustness.

Result: On VinDr-CXR: group-tail weighting + tail-aware thresholding reduced tail FNR from 0.665 to 0.269, sex worst-group FNR from 0.705 to 0.157, age worst-group FNR from 0.822 to 0.133, while increasing macro-mAP from 0.611 to 0.635. Paired bootstrap contrasts supported these reductions, and GroupDRO showed that aggregate group robustness alone doesn't eliminate rare subgroup misses.

Conclusion: The study supports a narrow but important claim: achieving rare-label fairness in chest X-ray classification depends jointly on the specific finding, subgroup characteristics, and operating threshold selection - not just on label frequency or overall ranking metrics alone. This highlights the need for comprehensive fairness audits before deployment.

Abstract: In chest X-ray (CXR) classification, acceptable ranking performance can still leave rare-positive patients below threshold, especially within subgroups. Across VinDr-CXR and MIMIC-CXR/CXR-LT, we use a diagnostic ladder to separate class-level long-tail losses, subgroup-aware weighting, group robustness, and threshold selection. On VinDr-CXR, group-tail weighting followed by tail-aware thresholding reduces tail FNR from 0.665 to 0.269, sex worst-group FNR from 0.705 to 0.157, and age worst-group FNR from 0.822 to 0.133, while macro-mAP increases from 0.611 to 0.635. On MIMIC-CXR/CXR-LT, the same score-to-threshold comparison reduces tail FNR from 0.866 to 0.741 and lowers worst-group FNR across sex, age, race, and insurance; residual missed-positive rates nevertheless remain high. Paired bootstrap contrasts on VinDr support the thresholded FNR reductions, and GroupDRO reference runs indicate that aggregate group robustness alone does not remove rare subgroup misses in this setting. The study supports a narrow audit claim: rare-label fairness in CXR depends jointly on the finding, subgroup, and operating threshold, not on label frequency or ranking metrics alone.

### [59] [LLT: Local Linear Transformer for PDE Operator Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07718)
*Oded Ovadia,Eli Turkel*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Local Linear Transformer (LLT) combines linear global attention with local spatial mixing for efficient PDE operator learning across various discretizations and mesh types.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard transformer attention has two major limitations for PDE applications: quadratic scaling with computational nodes and lack of explicit bias toward local interactions, which are important for physical systems.

Method: Introduces Local Linear Transformer (LLT) architecture that combines linear global attention with local spatial mixing, incorporating coordinate and geometry information to handle both long-range dependencies and local interactions efficiently.

Result: LLT achieves competitive or lower relative L2 error across multiple PDE problems (elasticity, plasticity, airfoil flow, pipe flow, Darcy flow) compared to other neural operators and transformers. It reduces wall-clock time per training iteration by factors of 1.8 to 2.5 relative to Transolver on structured discretizations and scales to 3D car aerodynamics with 32,186 unstructured mesh points per sample.

Conclusion: LLT provides an accurate and computationally efficient operator for PDE problems across different discretizations, mesh types, and problem settings, addressing key limitations of standard transformer approaches for PDE learning.

Abstract: Neural operators have become a common approach for learning PDE solution maps and accelerating numerical simulations. Transformer-based neural operators are of particular interest, since attention can learn long-range dependencies in the computational domain. However, standard attention has two major limitations when applied to PDEs: it scales quadratically with the number of computational nodes, and it lacks an explicit bias toward local interactions. To address these issues, we introduce Local Linear Transformer (LLT) for PDE operator learning. The architecture combines linear global attention with local spatial mixing, and incorporates coordinate and geometry information. We evaluate LLT on several PDE problems, including elasticity, plasticity, airfoil flow, pipe flow, and Darcy flow. The reference data for these problems span finite-element, finite-volume, and finite-difference discretizations on structured and unstructured meshes. Compared with other neural-operator and transformer baselines from prior studies, LLT achieves competitive or lower relative $L_2$ error across these problems. On matched structured discretizations, wall-clock time per training iteration is reduced by factors of 1.8 to 2.5 relative to Transolver. We also scale the approach and apply it to a three-dimensional car aerodynamics dataset with 32,186 unstructured mesh points per sample. Together, these results indicate that LLT provides an accurate and computationally efficient operator for PDE problems across discretizations, mesh types, and problem settings.

### [60] [ReCoLoRA: Spectrum-Aware Recursive Consolidation for Continual LLM Fine-Tuning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07719)
*Wentao Lu*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: ReCoLoRA introduces a recursive consolidation framework for continual fine-tuning that prevents catastrophic forgetting by re-decomposing current weights before each new task, allowing models to build upon previous tasks rather than overwriting them.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current LoRA-style methods for parameter-efficient fine-tuning suffer from catastrophic forgetting when applied to sequential tasks - each new task tends to overwrite previous ones because they keep stacking low-rank updates on the same frozen weights, preventing effective continual learning.

Method: ReCoLoRA uses a spectrum-aware framework: 1) Initializes adapters from randomized SVD of pretrained weights, 2) Selects per-layer effective ranks using an elbow criterion, 3) Adapts principal subspace before opening residual capacity, 4) Recursively re-decomposes current effective weights (not original ones) before each new task into frozen residual, slowly updated principal component, and fresh adapter.

Result: On a six-task continual GLUE sequence across four 7-8B backbone models, ReCoLoRA achieved the best final average score on three of the four backbones compared to LoRA, PiSSA, AdaLoRA, and DoRA baselines, while training fewer parameters. An oracle-routed task-bank variant serves as an upper bound.

Conclusion: ReCoLoRA provides an effective solution for continual fine-tuning that prevents catastrophic forgetting through recursive consolidation, enabling models to build cumulative knowledge across sequential tasks while maintaining parameter efficiency.

Abstract: Parameter-efficient fine-tuning adapts a large language model to one task cheaply, but across a task sequence LoRA-style methods keep stacking low-rank updates on the same frozen weight, so each new task tends to overwrite the previous ones. We present ReCoLoRA (Recursive Consolidation of Low-Rank Adapters), a spectrum-aware framework for continual fine-tuning: adapters are initialized from a randomized SVD of the pretrained weight, per-layer effective ranks are selected by an elbow criterion, and the principal subspace is adapted before residual capacity is opened. Before each new task, ReCoLoRA re-decomposes the current effective weight, rather than the original one, into a frozen residual, a slowly updated principal component, and a fresh adapter (recursive consolidation), so every task starts from the model that has already absorbed its predecessors. On a six-task continual GLUE sequence over four 7-8B backbones, ReCoLoRA attains the best final average score on three of the four backbones against rank-swept LoRA, PiSSA, AdaLoRA, and DoRA baselines while training fewer parameters; an oracle-routed task-bank variant serves as an upper bound under full task isolation. Code: https://github.com/bhqy666/ReCoLoRA.

</details>


### [61] [Omni-Sleep: A Sleep Foundation Model via Hierarchical Contrastive Learning of CNS--ANS Dynamic](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07720)
*Zhoujie Hou,Song Wang,Kexin Lou,Mo Wang,Chen Wei,Quanying Liu*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Omni-Sleep is a sleep foundation model that uses physiological organization (CNS/ANS partitioning) as prior knowledge for structured representation learning from multimodal polysomnography data, achieving superior performance in sleep staging and disease classification with improved generalization and robustness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing sleep foundation models fuse heterogeneous biosignals (EEG, EOG, EMG, ECG, respiration) without considering their physiological organization, missing the natural CNS/ANS partitioning that reflects sleep physiology dynamics.

Method: Uses CNS/ANS partition as physiological prior for topology-constrained representation learning with three objectives: 1) intra-system consistency within neural and cardio-respiratory signals, 2) inter-system synchronization aligning subsystem trajectories, and 3) latent-space masked temporal modeling for long-horizon sleep dynamics. Pre-trained on 100,000+ hours of multi-center multimodal PSG data.

Result: Outperforms strong foundation-model baselines in sleep staging and multi-disease classification across datasets and modality-ablation settings. Shows improved label efficiency, cross-dataset generalization, and robustness to missing modalities.

Conclusion: Physiological hierarchy provides valuable prior knowledge for generalizable sleep representation learning, enabling better performance, generalization, and robustness in sleep analysis tasks compared to topology-agnostic approaches.

Abstract: Sleep physiology arises from the coordinated dynamics of the central nervous system (CNS) and autonomic nervous system (ANS), as reflected by multimodal polysomnography signals including EEG, EOG, EMG, ECG, and respiration. However, existing sleep foundation models often fuse heterogeneous biosignals in a topology-agnostic manner, overlooking their physiological organization. We introduce Omni-Sleep, a sleep foundation model that uses the CNS/ANS partition as a physiological prior for topology-constrained representation learning. Omni-Sleep learns structured representations through three objectives: intra-system consistency, which captures shared subsystem-level factors within neural and cardio-respiratory signals; inter-system synchronization, which aligns subsystem trajectories to model brain--body dynamics; and latent-space masked temporal modeling, which captures long-horizon sleep dynamics. Pre-trained on over 100,000 hours of multi-center multimodal PSG data, Omni-Sleep is evaluated on sleep staging and multi-disease classification. Across datasets and modality-ablation settings, Omni-Sleep outperforms strong foundation-model baselines, showing improved label efficiency, cross-dataset generalization, and robustness to missing modalities. These results highlight the value of physiological hierarchy for generalizable sleep representation learning. Code is available at https://github.com/AutoBrain-sleep/OmniSleep.

</details>


### [62] [Uncertainty-gated selection for block-sparse attention](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07724)
*Thomas Rossi*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Block-sparse attention improvement: value-of-information router identifies indecisive top-k selections and doubles kept blocks for uncertain queries, boosting recall while maintaining efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard block-sparse attention with top-k selection is myopic - when the k-th and (k+1)-th blocks have nearly equal scores, the selector commits without extra budget, potentially dropping blocks containing crucial answer evidence that cannot be recovered downstream.

Method: Proposed value-of-information router measures how decisively the top-k cut was made for each query, then doubles the kept set for queries where the score gap between k-th and (k+1)-th blocks is smallest. Within 2 pp of dense performance on RULER NIAH multikey. Results reproduce across four models from three architectures. At 128K context, router preserves 0.81 and 0.89 of dense accuracy vs. SSA-style top-k at 0.09, while fused pipeline runs at 0.62x and 0.80x dense wall time.

Conclusion: The value-of-information router significantly improves block-sparse attention by adaptively allocating compute budget based on decision uncertainty, achieving near-dense performance with substantial speedups, addressing a critical limitation of myopic top-k selection in long-context language models.

Abstract: Block-sparse attention scales long-context language models by replacing the O(N^2) softmax with a per-query top-k selection over key blocks. This cutoff is myopic: when the k-th and (k+1)-th blocks are nearly tied in score, the selector commits without spending extra budget, and a dropped block carrying answer evidence is unrecoverable downstream. We propose a value-of-information router that measures, for each query, how decisively the top-k cut was made, and doubles the kept set for the queries where that gap is smallest; the rule is backbone-agnostic and stacks with existing block-scoring methods such as Quest. On LongBench-v2 medium at n=215 (the entire dataset subset), router-on-Quest reaches paired recall 0.75 vs. top-k 0.47 -- +28 pp over the SSA-style baseline (McNemar p<0.01) -- and lands within 2 pp of dense on RULER NIAH multikey at the same context. The lift reproduces on four models from three architectures (Qwen2.5, Mistral-Nemo, Qwen3.6). At 128K, the router preserves 0.81 and 0.89 of dense accuracy on Qwen2.5-7B-1M and Qwen3.6 (vs. SSA-style top-k at 0.09 on the former) while the fused selection-plus-kernel pipeline runs at 0.62x and 0.80x dense wall time.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Modern LLMs are increasingly used in long-context applications like RAG, repository-level coding, and agentic workflows that push inputs beyond pretraining windows. Existing zero-shot methods either sacrifice short-context fidelity with aggressive scaling or break down at long contexts with conservative scaling, creating a trade-off problem.

Method: Jet-Long pairs a local RoPE-faithful window with a long-range window whose rescaling factor dynamically adapts to current sequence length. On Qwen3 models up to 128K context, it outperforms RULER baseline by +2.03-+4.79 percentage points, achieves best accuracy on HELMET-RAG benchmark, and lowest PG-19 perplexity. It also generalizes to hybrid architectures like Jet-Nemotron for further improvement.

Conclusion: Jet-Long provides an effective, efficient, and hyperparameter-resilient solution for zero-shot context extension, recovering base model fidelity at short inputs while extrapolating cleanly at long contexts, making it practical for real-world deployment of LLMs in long-context applications.

Abstract: Modern LLMs are increasingly deployed in long-context applications such as retrieval-augmented generation, repository-level coding, and agentic workflows whose accumulated reasoning and tool traces routinely push the input an order of magnitude past the pretraining window, making zero-shot context extension the dominant deployment path for open-weight checkpoints. Most existing zero-shot methods fix a single rescaling factor up front, so an aggressive factor sacrifices short-context fidelity while a conservative one breaks down at long contexts. We propose Jet-Long, a tuning-free zero-shot method that pairs a local RoPE-faithful window with a long-range window whose rescaling factor adapts dynamically to the current sequence length, recovering the base model exactly at short inputs while extrapolating cleanly at long ones. An inclusion-exclusion attention merge and an on-the-fly RoPE correction rotation make the bifocal construction essentially free at inference; fused into a single CuTe kernel, long-context prefill reaches up to $1.39\times$ FA2 throughput on H100 (approaching the Hopper-only FA4), and single-batch generation incurs $\le 4\%$ overhead at every length. On Qwen3-1.7B/4B/8B up to 128K context, Jet-Long leads RULER by $+4.79$/$+2.18$/$+2.03$~pp over the strongest baseline at 1.7B/4B/8B, achieves the best overall accuracy on HELMET-RAG (a benchmark identified by HELMET as the most efficient predictor of downstream long-context performance) and attains the lowest PG-19 perplexity. Jet-Long also generalizes to hybrid attention architectures such as Jet-Nemotron for further long-context improvement without retraining, and remains hyperparameter-resilient for ease of deployment.

Method: The authors introduce Meta Neural Cellular Automata (MetaNCA), which learns local rules that iteratively update the weights of a task network using only local interactions on the computation graph. Biological neurons, through local interactions transmitted through synapses, are able to learn efficiently and can adapt their connections over an organism's lifespan. Motivated by these desirable properties of adaptability and local interaction, neural cellular automata (NCA) models have been successful at learning morphogenesis solely through local update rules, demonstrating stability over many updates and robustness to perturbations. In this work, we introduce Meta Neural Cellular Automata (MetaNCA), a framework that learns local rules which self-organize the weights of artificial neural networks. A learned rule network iteratively updates the weights of a task network using only local interactions on the computation graph. We propose a novel Weight Transformer architecture for the local rule network, which uses linear attention to aggregate signals from neighboring weights and hidden states. Once trained, the rule network generates task networks of diverse architectures without backpropagation. We show that MetaNCA generates weights for feedforward MLPs, CNNs, and ResNets on MNIST and CIFAR-100, scaling to networks of 2 million parameters. We further show that MetaNCA generalizes to architectures not seen during meta-training, and that architectural diversity in the training phase strengthens this generalization.

### [66] [LiST: Lipschitz Scaling Training for Robust and Calibrated Neural Networks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07745)
*Arthur Chiron,Franck Mamalet,Thomas Massena,Thomas Deltort,Mathieu Serrurier*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Lipschitz Scaling Training (LiST) is a novel training paradigm that automatically adjusts Lipschitz constraints to achieve calibrated networks that balance accuracy, robustness, and calibration simultaneously.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current neural networks struggle to achieve accuracy, robustness, and calibration simultaneously. Lipschitz-constrained models guarantee robustness but require manual selection of Lipschitz constraints, creating accuracy-robustness trade-offs and leaving calibration properties unexplored.

Method: The authors identify a theoretical link between Lipschitz constraints and Temperature Scaling calibration. They introduce Lipschitz Scaling Training (LiST), which iteratively adjusts the global Lipschitz constant to reach an optimal operating point. LiST includes a margin parameter to construct a calibrated Pareto front, and enables reintegration of calibration data into training.

Result: LiST achieves competitive accuracy and robustness on CIFAR-10/100 and Tiny-ImageNet while remaining calibrated out of the box, outperforming both constrained and unconstrained baselines.

Conclusion: Lipschitz Scaling Training provides a principled approach to simultaneously achieve accuracy, robustness, and calibration in neural networks, enabling users to navigate the accuracy-robustness trade-off while maintaining calibration throughout.

Abstract: While accuracy, robustness, and calibration are all essential for reliable neural networks, they are often studied separately; developing models that satisfy all three simultaneously remains a central challenge. Lipschitz-constrained models guarantee robustness by design, yet the manual selection of the Lipschitz constraint L governs the resulting accuracy-robustness trade-off, and their calibration properties remain largely underexplored. In this work, we highlight a theoretical and empirical link between the enforced Lipschitz constraint and Temperature Scaling, a state-of-the-art calibration method. Specifically, we find that for a given training scheme, there exists a non-trivial value L* that yields an out-of-the-box calibrated network, and that calibration acts as a principled criterion to select a well-defined operating point on the accuracy-robustness Pareto front. Leveraging these insights, we introduce Lipschitz Scaling Training (LiST), a novel training paradigm that iteratively adjusts the global Lipschitz constant to reach this operating point. Through a margin parameter in the training loss, LiST further enables the construction of a fully calibrated Pareto front, allowing users to navigate the accuracy-robustness trade-off while remaining calibrated throughout. At convergence, LiST also enables the reintegration of calibration data into training, improving sample efficiency without sacrificing calibration. We validate LiST on CIFAR-10/100 and Tiny-ImageNet, demonstrating competitive accuracy and robustness against constrained and unconstrained baselines, while remaining calibrated out of the box. Code is available at GitHub.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Large Language Models perform well on high-resource programming languages like Python and Java but suffer sharp performance drops on Low-Resource Programming Languages (LRPLs) like Julia. Small Language Models face a trilemma: SFT is bottlenecked by data scarcity, inference-time scaling is too expensive for deployment, and Reinforcement Learning from scratch yields near zero advantages.

Method: Three-phase pipeline: 1) Offline data synthesis using iterative compiler and test feedback to generate verified training examples (left-shifting compute), 2) Fine-tuning an SLM on synthetic verified data to embed strong syntactic priors, 3) Applying Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Reward (RLVR) grounded by language-agnostic Input/Output tests, where the SFT prior constrains exploration away from syntax errors.

Result: Applied to Qwen3-8B, improves pass@1 by up to +7.6 points on MultiPL-E and +14.2 points on Agnostics LiveCodeBench for Julia compared to SOTA. The pipeline used only 1/3 data and 1/6 cost over previous SOTA. Generalizes to Ballerina achieving 49.7% MultiPL-E Pass@1 (language with near-zero pretraining representation). Ablations confirm both SFT phase and execution-grounded rewards are necessary for stable training.

Conclusion: The proposed three-phase pipeline effectively resolves the trilemma of LRPL code generation by decoupling syntax acquisition from algorithmic reasoning, achieving significant performance improvements with substantially reduced data and computational costs, while demonstrating generalization to languages with minimal pretraining representation.

Abstract: Large Language Models achieve strong code generation for high resource languages like Python and Java but suffer sharp performance drops on Low-Resource Programming Languages~(LRPLs) such as Julia. Improving Small Language Models~(SLMs) for these languages faces a trilemma: Supervised Fine-Tuning~(SFT) is bottlenecked by data scarcity, inference-time scaling is too expensive for deployment, and Reinforcement Learning from scratch yields near zero advantages. We propose a three-phase pipeline that resolves this trilemma by decoupling syntax acquisition from algorithmic reasoning. First, we \emph{left-shift} inference-time compute to an offline data synthesis engine that uses iterative compiler and test feedback to generate verified training examples. Second, we fine-tune an SLM on this synthetic, verified data to embed strong syntactic priors. Third, we apply Reinforcement Learning with Verifiable Reward~(RLVR) grounded by language-agnostic Input/Output tests, where the SFT prior constrains exploration away from syntax errors. Applied to Qwen3-8B, our pipeline improves pass@1 by up to +7.6 points on MultiPL-E and +14.2 points on the Agnostics LiveCodeBench for Julia compared to SOTA results. Furthermore, the pipeline only used $\frac{1}{3}$ data and $\frac{1}{6}$ cost over the previous state-of-the-art. We further demonstrate that the pipeline generalizes to Ballerina achieving 49.7\% MultiPL-E Pass@1, a language with near-zero pretraining representation. Ablations confirm that both the SFT phase and execution-grounded rewards are necessary for stable training.

### [68] [A Transdiagnostic Space of Disorder Like Phenotypes in Reinforcement Learning Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07753)
*Hari Prasad*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current approaches to modeling psychological disorders in RL agents are limited: they induce only 1-2 disorders via hand-tuned reward shaping, label behavior post-hoc, and report single runs. The authors aim to develop a more systematic framework that can model multiple disorders as controllable manipulations grounded in computational psychiatry accounts.

Method: Reformulate disorder modeling as dose-controllable manipulation of cognitive appraisal signals in an appraisal-guided PPO agent. Represent seven disorders (anxiety, mania, OCD checking, depression, impulsivity, addiction, PTSD) each as a single "knob" parameter grounded in computational psychiatry theory. Use preregistered assays mapped to recognized psychological paradigms to measure symptoms. Conduct extensive evaluation across more than a thousand runs with 10 seeds and 95% confidence intervals.

Result: Every disorder shows graded, monotone dose-response relationships that control conditions cannot reproduce. Three emergent findings: (1) disorders self-organize into a two-dimensional affective space where mania mirrors anxiety; (2) removing knobs remits reward distortion disorders but not avoidance disorders, which require graded exposure for recovery; (3) simultaneous knobs interact nonadditively, yielding testable comorbidity predictions. The framework successfully transfers to 3D pixel environments with standard convolutional agents.

Conclusion: Appraisal weights parameterize a controllable space of affective phenotypes where the same knobs that induce disorders can also model their treatment. The framework generalizes beyond grid worlds and specific PPO architecture, providing a principled approach for computational psychiatry research and understanding failure modes in affective control systems.

Abstract: Modelling psychological disorders in artificial agents offers both a testbed for computational psychiatry and a lens on the failure modes of affective control. Prior work induces one or two disorders in a reinforcement learning (RL) agent by hand-tuned reward shaping, labels the behaviour post hoc, and reports single runs. We recast disorder modelling as dose-controllable manipulation of cognitive appraisal signals in an appraisal-guided PPO agent, expressing seven disorders (anxiety, mania, obsessive-compulsive checking, depression, impulsivity, addiction, and post-traumatic stress) each as a single knob grounded in a computational psychiatry account, with each symptom measured by a preregistered assay mapped to a recognised paradigm. Across more than a thousand runs (10 seeds, four controls, 95% confidence intervals) every disorder shows a graded, monotone dose-response that no control reproduces. Beyond these induced effects, three findings emerge that were not written into the reward: the disorders self-organise into a two-dimensional affective space in which mania mirrors anxiety; removing a knob remits reward distortion disorders (mania, checking, addiction) but not avoidance disorders (anxiety, PTSD), which instead recover under a graded exposure curriculum; and two simultaneous knobs interact nonadditively, yielding testable comorbidity predictions. Appraisal weights thus parameterise a controllable space of affective phenotypes in which the same knobs that induce a disorder can model its treatment. We also show that three disorder knobs (depression, addiction, anxiety) transfer to a three-dimensional pixel environment (MiniWorld) with a standard convolutional agent and no appraisal critic, with cross-assay dissociation confirmed across both domains, indicating the framework is not specific to grid worlds or to PPO's appraisal critic.

The classical networks use diffusion-based smearing and block-wise pooling to downsample the image data and capture important structural features. In this work, we propose and demonstrate a more efficient quantum-inspired strategy involving a mixture of experts. It is a hybrid classical-quantum framework. The quantum part consists of amplitude encoding of the images, convolution using local unitary operations, multiple experts processing the same image with different parameters, and feature extraction using quantum stabiliser codes. The classical part then jointly processes the features extracted by different experts using a standard fully connected neural network for image class prediction. Using MNIST and Fashion-MNIST datasets as benchmarks, we demonstrate that the joint expert analysis outperforms the individual expert one, as well as reduces the failure rate of image class prediction by around a factor of two. The overhead of our quantum-inspired strategy is only moderate on GPU workstations, which makes our proposal a practical alternative to existing classical schemes. We also point out how the quantum part of our framework can be executed on a quantum processor.

Method: The paper analyzes Hamiltonian Generative Networks (HGN) in externally forced, dissipative environments, identifying two failure modes for temporal generalization: latent magnitude growth from unconstrained action-force maps and global truncation error from under-resolved integrators. It proposes targeted fixes for each mechanism.

Result: The proposed targeted fixes enable stable dynamics prediction at temporal resolutions well outside the training distribution, overcoming HGN's temporal generalization breakdown in non-conservative settings.

Conclusion: The paper recommends several strategies for enabling temporal generalization in continuous-time video generation, addressing a key limitation of current world models and opening pathways for more flexible dynamical prediction across multiple timescales.

Abstract: World models are typically trained to predict discrete-time physical dynamics with a fixed step size baked into the model weights, preventing prediction at variable temporal resolutions. This matters for hierarchical planning, sim-to-real transfer, and scientific or game-engine applications that must query the same dynamics at multiple timescales. Hamiltonian Generative Networks (HGN) offer a principled path forward, grounding predictions in a continuous-time energy function that is, in principle, independent of the observation frame rate. In practice, however, their temporal generalization breaks down in non-conservative settings. We show that in externally forced, dissipative environments, HGN rollouts at step sizes beyond the training regime fail due to distinct failure modes, including latent magnitude growth driven by an unconstrained action-force map, and global truncation error accumulation from an under-resolved integrator. We identify a targeted fix for each mechanism and demonstrate stable dynamics prediction at temporal resolutions well outside the training distribution. In a detailed analysis, we recommend several strategies for enabling temporal generalization in continuous-time video generation.

### [76] [A law of robustness for two-layer neural networks with arbitrary weights](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07778)
*Yitzchak Shmalo*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: The paper proves a lower bound on Lipschitz constant for two-layer neural networks fitting noisy data: Lip(f) ≥ cε√[n/(m log(Cmnd/ε))] with high probability, confirming a conjecture by Bubeck, Li and Nagaraj up to a logarithmic factor.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Bubeck, Li and Nagaraj conjectured that for generic data, any two-layer neural network with m neurons fitting n noisy labels must have Lipschitz constant at least of order √(n/m), with no weight restrictions. While Bubeck and Sellke proved a version for bounded parameters, the unbounded-weight case requires different arguments, especially since at depth three, boundedness is necessary.

Method: The proof uses function-space covering instead of parameter-space covering (impossible for unbounded weights). The key deterministic ingredient is a rigidity lemma: on B₂ ball and S^{d-1} for d≥3, the coefficient of each canonical kink is controlled by the Lipschitz constant because kinks on distinct hyperplanes cannot cancel at generic points. The approach handles continuous piecewise-linear activations like ReLU.

Result: For data from S^{d-1} (d≥3) or N(0,I_d/d), labels in [-1,1] with noise σ²>0, and any width-m two-layer network with arbitrary real weights/biases/affine skip connection, fitting data ε below noise floor forces Lip(f) ≥ cε√[n/(m̄ log(Cm̄nd/ε))] with high probability, where m̄ = (K-1)m+1 (K being piece count per neuron). A realized-kink-count version also holds.

Conclusion: The paper confirms the conjectured law (up to logarithmic factor) for two-layer piecewise-linear networks with unbounded weights, showing that fitting noisy data forces large Lipschitz constant. The result highlights the importance of the rigidity property (which fails at d=2), and demonstrates that at overparameterization endpoint, an explicit interpolant with O(1) Lipschitz constant exists.

Abstract: Bubeck, Li and Nagaraj conjectured that, for generic data, any two-layer neural network with $m$ neurons that fits $n$ noisy labels must have Lipschitz constant at least of order $\sqrt{n/m}$, with no restriction on the size of the weights. Bubeck and Sellke proved a universal version of this law for Lipschitz-parameterized classes, but under a polynomial bound on the parameters; at depth three that boundedness hypothesis is genuinely necessary. The two-layer unbounded-weight case requires a different argument. We prove the conjectured law, up to one logarithmic factor, for every continuous piecewise-linear activation, in particular for ReLU networks. For data drawn uniformly from $\mathbb{S}^{d-1}$, $d\ge3$, or from $N(0,I_d/d)$, labels in $[-1,1]$ with noise level $σ^2>0$, and any width-$m$ two-layer network with arbitrary real weights, biases and affine skip connection, fitting the data $\varepsilon$ below the noise floor forces $\mathrm{Lip}(f)\ge c\,\varepsilon\sqrt{n/(\bar m\log(C\bar m nd/\varepsilon))}$, $\bar m=(K-1)m+1$, with high probability. A realized-kink-count version holds on the same event: every realized two-layer piecewise-linear function with $k(f)\le n$ distinct kink hyperplanes obeys the bound with $\bar m$ replaced by $k(f)+1$, irrespective of how many redundant hidden units parameterize it. The proof replaces parameter-space covering, impossible for unbounded weights, by a function-space covering. The central deterministic ingredient is a rigidity lemma: on $B_2$, and on $\mathbb{S}^{d-1}$ for $d\ge3$, the coefficient of each canonical kink is controlled by the Lipschitz constant of the realized function, because kinks on distinct hyperplanes cannot cancel at generic points. Rigidity genuinely fails at $d=2$, and an explicit two-layer ReLU interpolant with $O(1)$ Lipschitz constant at width $2n$ matches the law at the overparameterized endpoint.

</details>

Ensemble tree-based methods achieved the strongest discrimination: Random Forest reached 0.74 +/- 0.05 with environmental predictors; Extra Trees reached 0.77 +/- 0.06 with biological variables added. ### [78] [Explaining Near-Zero Hessian Eigenvalues Through Approximate Symmetries in Neural Networks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07845)
*Marcel Kühn,Bernd Rosenow*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: The paper explains that the many near-zero eigenvalues in neural network Hessians originate from weakly broken continuous symmetries of network parametrization, which create pseudo-Goldstone modes.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Despite existing understanding of large Hessian eigenvalues, the origin of the vast number of vanishingly small eigenvalues in neural network loss landscapes remains unexplained. The authors aim to clarify why there are so many near-zero modes in the Hessian spectrum.

Method: 1) Analyze deep linear networks where continuous symmetries generate exact flat directions (zero modes). 2) Introduce ReLU nonlinearity as a perturbation that weakly breaks these symmetries. 3) Examine eigenvector structure to show high-curvature directions are orthogonal to symmetry subspace while bulk lies within it. 4) Demonstrate mechanism in a two-layer ReLU student-teacher model and network trained on CIFAR-10. 5) Extend analysis to convolutional layers.

Result: The Hessian bulk consists of weakly lifted pseudo-Goldstone modes from broken continuous symmetries. High-curvature directions are orthogonal to symmetry subspace, while near-zero modes lie almost entirely within the symmetry subspace. The mechanism holds in practical networks (CIFAR-10 trained models) and extends beyond fully connected layers to convolutional networks.

Conclusion: The multitude of small Hessian eigenvalues originates from weakly broken continuous symmetries of network parametrization, linking the Hessian bulk to pseudo-Goldstone modes and providing a clear explanation for near-zero modes in neural network loss landscapes.

Abstract: The Hessian of the training loss governs the local geometry of the loss landscape, yet despite existing explanations for its largest eigenvalues, the origin of the vast multitude of vanishingly small eigenvalues remains elusive. We argue that the bulk consists of the weakly lifted pseudo-Goldstone modes of the continuous symmetries of the network parametrization. In deep linear networks these symmetries are exact: they generate flat directions and hence exact zero modes, whose eigenvectors we construct explicitly. Introducing a ReLU nonlinearity as a perturbation, we show that it breaks these symmetries weakly and explicitly. Resolving the spectrum at the level of eigenvectors, we find that the high-curvature directions are orthogonal to the symmetry subspace, while the bulk lies almost entirely within it. We demonstrate the mechanism in a two-layer ReLU student--teacher model and in a network trained on CIFAR-10. A convolutional example demonstrates that the same diagnostic extends beyond fully connected layers. Together, these results link the Hessian bulk to weakly broken symmetries and clarify the origin of near-zero modes.

### [79] [When Does Continual Learning Require Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07847)
*Anne Harrington,Nayan Saxena,Michael Murphy,Anastasia Borovykh,Zeyu Yun,Sridhar Kamath,Ara Eindra Kyi,Trevor Darrell,Jitendra Malik,Yutong Bai*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: The paper argues that continual learning for LLMs isn't just about managing context and avoiding forgetting, but about increasing model competence as the world changes across spatial (new domains) and temporal (data drift) axes, evaluating different methods under realistic sequential conditions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current framing of continual learning for LLMs focuses too narrowly on context management and forgetting prevention. The paper argues this is incomplete because continual learning should fundamentally increase model competence as the world evolves, which involves encountering new domains over time (spatial axis) and dealing with data drift under fixed tasks (temporal axis).

Method: The authors disentangle continual learning along spatial (new domains) and temporal (data drift) axes, creating a realistic setting where new domains arrive over time, facts drift past training cutoff, and agentic interactions accumulate state. They recast widely used LLM benchmarks as sequential problems and introduce a mechanism-agnostic protocol comparing prompt-based methods (GEPA, ACE), supervised learning (SFT, SDFT), reinforcement learning (GRPO, SDPO), and context compression (Cartridges, In-place TTT).

Result: Prompt-based methods fit new stages quickly but degrade on future tasks. Distillation methods accumulate knowledge stably but struggle to update outdated facts. Context compression improves efficiency without substantially improving learning ability. Online reinforcement learning adapts most effectively to knowledge updates but remains sensitive to noisy reward signals. Different environmental change patterns require different update behaviors.

Conclusion: Continual learning is not a single capability - different patterns of environmental change require fundamentally different update behaviors, determining when adaptation must be learned inside model weights versus when it can be achieved through external scaffolding. Understanding where each method succeeds and fails will guide stronger continual learning system design.

Abstract: As large language models (LLMs) become increasingly capable, the next question is how can we enable models to continually learn? Today, the field largely frames this as a problem of context management and mitigating forgetting. We argue this framing is incomplete: continual learning is fundamentally about increasing model competence as the world changes. We disentangle this change along two axes -- space, where the model encounters new domains, and time, where the underlying data drifts under a fixed task. This framing lets us study continual learning under realistic conditions: new domains arrive over time, facts drift past their training cutoff, and agentic interactions accumulate state across episodes. To evaluate methods under this setting, we recast widely used LLM benchmarks as sequential problems and introduce a single mechanism-agnostic protocol that compares prompt-based methods (GEPA, ACE), supervised learning (SFT, SDFT), reinforcement learning (GRPO, SDPO), and context compression (Cartridges, In-place TTT). Prompt-based methods fit each new stage quickly but degrade on future tasks. Distillation-based methods accumulate knowledge stably but struggle to update outdated facts. Context compression improves efficiency without substantially improving the ability to learn new tasks. Online reinforcement learning adapts most effectively to knowledge updates but remains sensitive to noisy reward signals. Overall, our results suggest that continual learning is not a single capability: different patterns of environmental change require fundamentally different update behaviors, determining when adaptation must be learned inside model weights and when it can be achieved through external scaffolding. We hope that understanding where each method succeeds and fails will guide the design of stronger continual learning systems.

</details>


### [80] [NFTR: From Provable Mode-Averaging to Geodesic Subgoal Selection in Offline Goal-Conditioned RL](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07855)
*Erdemt Bao,Xing Lei,Jun Chen*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: NFTR addresses failure modes in hierarchical offline RL by replacing Gaussian subgoal policies with Normalizing Flows and using triangle slack reweighting to avoid optimistic bias and mode collapse.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The paper identifies two key failure modes in Hierarchical Implicit Q-Learning (HIQL): optimistic bias (treating lucky stochastic outcomes as skillful choices) and mode collapse (reducing multi-modal subgoal distributions to single Gaussian means that fall in unreachable regions). These issues limit the effectiveness of existing offline goal-conditioned RL methods.

Method: NFTR combines two key components: 1) A conditional Normalizing Flow replaces the Gaussian policy for subgoal selection, providing minimal generative capability for AWR-based selection, and 2) A triangle slack score multiplicatively corrects the AWR weight to downweight subgoals whose detour cost exceeds average reachability. The method also introduces the RWDR objective that preserves population-level monotonic improvement.

Result: The approach yields subgoal selection that provably avoids Gaussian collapse and remains stable under stochastic dynamics. The triangle slack vanishes on geodesics in deterministic MDPs and provides conservative upper bounds on composability violation under stochastic dynamics.

Conclusion: NFTR successfully addresses the identified failure modes in hierarchical offline RL by combining Normalizing Flow subgoal policies with triangle slack reweighting, providing theoretically sound improvements over existing methods like HIQL for offline goal-conditioned reinforcement learning.

Abstract: Hierarchical Implicit Q-Learning (HIQL), an offline goal-conditioned RL method, selects subgoals by value-function advantages alone. This rule has two coupled failure modes. Optimistic bias treats lucky stochastic outcomes as skillful choices, and mode collapse reduces a multi-modal subgoal distribution to a single Gaussian mean that often falls in unreachable regions. We propose NFTR (Normalizing Flows subgoal policies with Triangle-slack Reweighting). A conditional Normalizing Flow replaces the Gaussian policy, and a closed-form mode-averaging result identifies NFs as the minimal generative class for AWR-based subgoal selection. A triangle slack score, built on the architectural triangle inequality without relying on distance accuracy, multiplicatively corrects the AWR weight to downweight subgoals whose detour cost exceeds average reachability. Triangle-slack vanishes on geodesics in deterministic MDPs and remains a conservative upper bound on composability violation under stochastic dynamics. The RWDR objective preserves AWR's population-level monotonic improvement and admits a three-term suboptimality decomposition. Together, these two ingredients yield subgoal selection that provably avoids the Gaussian collapse described above and remains stable under stochastic dynamics. GitHub page: https://github.com/erdemtbao/NFTR

*Sarah Grewe,Jörg Frochte*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A methodological study on small experimental datasets in machining processes shows that data curation strategies, robust evaluation methods, and careful physics integration are more important than choice of learning algorithm for reliable modeling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In physically dominated machining processes, experimental datasets are small, expensive, and material-specific, making data curation, evaluation design, and physics integration critical for reliable modeling. The paper addresses the challenge of developing robust machine learning approaches for such resource-constrained experimental settings.

Method: The study uses an abrasive waterjet milling dataset (n=155, Inconel 718) and makes three methodological contributions: (1) separating physics-based data cleaning from statistical curation, treating curation as competing modeling hypotheses; (2) analyzing evaluation stability via 15-point hold-out vs. 10-fold cross-validation; (3) studying a spectrum of physics integration levels from pure data-driven to physics-informed models, comparing Gaussian Process variants, gradient boosting, SVR, and tree-based models with Bayesian hyperparameter tuning.

Result: Model rankings from 15-point hold-out were unstable - the single-split winner dropped from rank 1 to rank 7 under 10-fold cross-validation. Gaussian Process variants consistently occupied top ranks. Residual learning on compact physics baselines worked well for GPs (lower variance, interpretable decomposition) but degraded tree-based models. Bayesian hyperparameter tuning helped parameter-sensitive baselines like gradient boosting and SVR but harmed multi-stage hybrid pipelines at small sample sizes. GP uncertainty intervals showed reasonable calibration (86% empirical coverage at nominal 90%).

Conclusion: For small, expensive process datasets, reliable model comparison benefits from explicit curation hypotheses, robust evaluation methods, and careful choices about physics integration rather than focusing solely on algorithm selection. The results suggest methodological considerations often outweigh algorithmic differences in such resource-constrained experimental settings.

Abstract: In physically dominated machining processes, experimental datasets are small, expensive, and material-specific; in this regime, data curation, evaluation design, and the form of physics integration can matter as much as the learning algorithm. Using an abrasive waterjet milling dataset ($n{=}155$, Inconel\,718), we make three methodological contributions. First, we separate physics-based data \emph{cleaning} from statistical \emph{curation} and treat the latter as competing modelling hypotheses rather than silent preprocessing. Second, we find that model rankings from a 15-point hold-out set can be unstable: the single-split winner drops from rank~1 to rank~7 under 10-fold cross-validation, while Gaussian Process (GP) variants occupy the top ranks. Third, we study a spectrum of physics integration levels and find that residual learning on a compact physics baseline is competitive for GP, yielding lower variance and an interpretable decomposition, but degrades tree-based models. Bayesian hyper parameter tuning improves parameter-sensitive baselines such as gradient boosting and SVR, yet harms multi-stage hybrid pipelines at this sample size. GP uncertainty intervals are approximately calibrated ($86\%$ empirical coverage at nominal $90\%$). The resulting picture is methodological: for small, expensive process datasets, our results suggest that, in this setting, reliable model comparison benefits from explicit curation hypotheses, robust evaluation, and careful choices about how physics enters the model.

### [82] [Optimal Learning Rate Scaling Depends on Data in Deep Scalar Linear Networks](https://arxiv.org/abs/2607.07884)
*Yedi Zhang,Peter E. Latham,Leena Chennuru Vankadara,Andrew Saxe*

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Analyzed gradient descent dynamics of deep scalar linear networks (f(x) = ∏_{l=1}^L w_l x) which have exact time-course solutions for any integer depth. Examined optimal depth-wise learning rate scaling under different data conditions, and extended analysis to networks with residual connections.

Result: 1) Optimal learning rate scaling depends on data properties, 2) Data-agnostic scaling rules fail to transfer across depths, 3) With data-dependent optimal scaling, learning dynamics become data-independent and weakly depth-dependent, 4) Achieves constant linear convergence rate across all depths (including infinite depth), 5) Similar effects observed in networks with residual connections.

Conclusion: The analysis reveals fundamental limitations of data-agnostic learning rate scaling rules for deep networks and demonstrates that proper data-dependent scaling can lead to depth-independent convergence properties, challenging conventional wisdom about depth-dependent optimization difficulties.

Abstract: In this short note we consider the gradient descent dynamics of deep scalar linear networks, $f(x) = \prod_{l=1}^L w_l x$, which enjoy exact time-course solutions for any integer depth. We show that even in this minimal model, the optimal depth-wise learning rate scaling depends on data, whereas data-agnostic scaling rules fail to transfer across depths. Under the data-dependent optimal scaling, the learning dynamics is independent of data and weakly dependent on depth, resulting in a constant linear convergence rate across all depths including infinity. We further show similar data-dependent effects in deep scalar linear networks with residual connections.

Method: Replace explicit harm refusal with adversarial training on 66 harm-agnostic personality statements from psychometric literature, hypothesizing that personality-anchored representations share latent structure with harm avoidance.

Conclusion: LPA demonstrates that lightweight, harm-agnostic adversarial training using personality statements can provide robust jailbreak defense without compromising utility or requiring large harmful datasets.

Abstract: Current safety methods for large language models are known to be vulnerable to adversarial attacks, motivating research into robust alternatives. Latent Adversarial Training (LAT) is among the most effective defenses, but can degrade utility and requires training on large datasets of harmful prompts. We introduce Latent Personality Alignment (LPA), which replaces explicit harm refusal with adversarial training on just 66 harm-agnostic statements drawn from psychometric personality literature. We hypothesize that personality-anchored representations share latent structure with harm avoidance, so adversarially stabilizing them implicitly constrains the subspace exploited by jailbreak attacks. LPA achieves near-zero attack success rates on HarmBench across direct requests and five jailbreak methods, despite never seeing harmful content during training and no loss of performance on standard benchmarks. Moreover, the training process is lightweight; the entire procedure completes in minutes on a single GPU and uses 75x fewer examples than standard LAT. Extensive ablations demonstrate the robustness, efficiency, and generalization of our method.

LoRA fine-tuning substantially improves both adapted families and largely repairs this instability, yet no foundation model surpasses the trained recurrent baselines on any metric. These findings challenge the assumption that larger pretrained models universally dominate environmental forecasting and provide actionable deployment guidance for wildfire air quality prediction.

Hybrid stacks improved loss but reduced throughput. Cross-Layer Value Routing (CLVR) modestly improved performance for both DeltaNet and Gated DeltaNet.

Conclusion: Recurrent linear-attention architectures offer practical alternatives to quadratic self-attention, with different tradeoffs in performance, throughput, and memory management. Abstract: Self-attention lets each token retrieve information from the full context, but its quadratic cost in sequence length limits training and inference at long context. The reported speed results measure training throughput and iteration time; we do not provide an empirical inference-speed benchmark. Within the reported 350M-parameter, 15B-token sweep, Kimi Delta Attention with Muon reaches the lowest final validation loss, a pure Gated DeltaNet stack trained with AdamW has the highest normalized training throughput, hybrid stacks generally improve loss at a throughput cost, and Muon consistently lowers final validation loss relative to AdamW in the matched architecture settings we evaluate. We introduce and evaluate lightweight cross-layer routing mechanisms for DeltaNet-style memories. The most natural DeltaNet-inspired formulation, forwarding a lower layer's delta-rule write error into the next layer's value target, does not improve over matched baselines. Routing into the aligned hidden stream and forwarding the write value instead yields a modest improvement in the matched runs we report: Cross-Layer Value Routing (CLVR) lowers final validation loss for both DeltaNet and Gated DeltaNet.
